<p><a href="/wiki/Geir_Lundestad" title="Geir Lundestad">Geir Lundestad</a>, 2006 Secretary of Norwegian Nobel Committee, said, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The greatest omission in our 106 year history is undoubtedly that Mahatma Gandhi never received the Nobel Peace Prize. Gandhi could do without the Nobel Peace Prize. Whether Nobel committee can do without Gandhi is the question.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Other high-profile individuals with widely recognised contributions to peace have been overlooked. In 2009, an article in <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy" title="Foreign Policy">Foreign Policy</a></i> magazine identified seven people who "never won the prize, but should have". The list consisted of Gandhi, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Roosevelt" title="Eleanor Roosevelt">Eleanor Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A1clav_Havel" title="Václav Havel">Václav Havel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Saro-Wiwa" title="Ken Saro-Wiwa">Ken Saro-Wiwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sari_Nusseibeh" title="Sari Nusseibeh">Sari Nusseibeh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corazon_Aquino" title="Corazon Aquino">Corazon Aquino</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liu_Xiaobo" title="Liu Xiaobo">Liu Xiaobo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_Also_Rans_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel_Also_Rans-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Liu Xiaobo would go on to win the <a href="/wiki/2010_Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="2010 Nobel Peace Prize">2010 Nobel Peace Prize</a> while imprisoned.
</p><p>In 1965, UN Secretary General <a href="/wiki/U_Thant" title="U Thant">U Thant</a> was informed by the Norwegian Permanent Representative to the UN that he would be awarded that year's prize and asked whether or not he would accept. He consulted staff and later replied that he would. At the same time, Chairman <a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Jahn" title="Gunnar Jahn">Gunnar Jahn</a> of the Nobel Peace prize committee, lobbied heavily against giving U Thant the prize and the prize was at the last minute awarded to <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a>. The rest of the committee all wanted the prize to go to U Thant, for his work in defusing the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a>, ending the war in the Congo, and his ongoing work to mediate an end to the Vietnam War. The disagreement lasted three years and in 1966 and 1967 no prize was given, with Gunnar Jahn effectively vetoing an award to U Thant.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg/170px-Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg/255px-Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg/340px-Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="764" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Revolutionary_Joyce_Better_Contrast.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>, one of the controversial omissions of the Literature Prize</div></div></div>
<p>The Literature Prize also has controversial omissions. <a href="/wiki/Adam_Kirsch" title="Adam Kirsch">Adam Kirsch</a> has suggested that many notable writers have missed out on the award for political or extra-literary reasons. The heavy focus on European and Swedish authors has been a subject of criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-KirschNoClue_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KirschNoClue-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The Eurocentric nature of the award was acknowledged by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Englund" title="Peter Englund">Peter Englund</a>, the 2009 Permanent Secretary of the Swedish Academy, as a problem with the award and was attributed to the tendency for the academy to relate more to European authors.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> This tendency towards European authors still leaves many European writers on a list of notable writers that have been overlooked for the Literature Prize, including <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Chekhov" title="Anton Chekhov">Anton Chekhov</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Zola" title="Émile Zola">Émile Zola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Proust" title="Marcel Proust">Marcel Proust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">August Strindberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simon_Vestdijk" title="Simon Vestdijk">Simon Vestdijk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek" title="Karel Čapek">Karel Čapek</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Updike" title="John Updike">John Updike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Miller" title="Arthur Miller">Arthur Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>, and Africa's <a href="/wiki/Chinua_Achebe" title="Chinua Achebe">Chinua Achebe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman56-57_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman56-57-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Candidates can receive multiple nominations the same year. <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Ramon" title="Gaston Ramon">Gaston Ramon</a> received a total of 155<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> nominations in physiology or medicine from 1930 to 1953, the last year with public nomination data for that award as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nobel_Prize&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>. He died in 1963 without being awarded. <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Paul_%C3%89mile_Roux" class="mw-redirect" title="Pierre Paul Émile Roux">Pierre Paul Émile Roux</a> received 115<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> nominations in physiology or medicine, and <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Sommerfeld" title="Arnold Sommerfeld">Arnold Sommerfeld</a> received 84<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> in physics. These are the three most nominated scientists without awards in the data published as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nobel_Prize&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Otto_Stern" title="Otto Stern">Otto Stern</a> received 79<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> nominations in physics 1925–1943 before being awarded in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The strict rule against awarding a prize to more than three people is also controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-Levinovitz61_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levinovitz61-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> When a prize is awarded to recognise an achievement by a team of more than three collaborators, one or more will miss out. For example, in 2002, the prize was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Koichi_Tanaka" title="Koichi Tanaka">Koichi Tanaka</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Fenn_(chemist)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Fenn (chemist)">John Fenn</a> for the development of <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> in <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein chemistry</a>, an award that did not recognise the ac
</p><p>hievements of <a href="/wiki/Franz_Hillenkamp" title="Franz Hillenkamp">Franz Hillenkamp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Karas" title="Michael Karas">Michael Karas</a> of the Institute for Physical and Theoretical Chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/Goethe_University_Frankfurt" title="Goethe University Frankfurt">University of Frankfurt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Spinney_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spinney-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> According to one of the nominees for the prize in physics, the three person limit deprived him and two other members of his team of the honor in 2013: the team of <a href="/wiki/C._R._Hagen" title="C. R. Hagen">Carl Hagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Guralnik" title="Gerald Guralnik">Gerald Guralnik</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kibble" title="Tom Kibble">Tom Kibble</a> published a paper in 1964 that gave answers to how the cosmos began, but did not share the 2013 Physics Prize awarded to <a href="/wiki/Peter_Higgs" title="Peter Higgs">Peter Higgs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Englert" title="François Englert">François Englert</a>, who had also published papers in 1964 concerning the subject. All five physicists arrived at the same conclusion, albeit from different angles. Hagen contends that an equitable solution is to either abandon the three limit restriction, or expand the time period of recognition for a given achievement to two years.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, the prohibition of posthumous awards fails to recognise achievements by an individual or collaborator who dies before the prize is awarded. The Economics Prize was not awarded to <a href="/wiki/Fischer_Black" title="Fischer Black">Fischer Black</a>, who died in 1995, when his co-author <a href="/wiki/Myron_Scholes" title="Myron Scholes">Myron Scholes</a> received the honor in 1997 for their landmark work on option pricing along with <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Merton" title="Robert C. Merton">Robert C. Merton</a>, another pioneer in the development of valuation of stock options. In the announcement of the award that year, the Nobel committee prominently mentioned Black's key role.
</p><p>Political subterfuge may also deny proper recognition. <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a>, who co-discovered nuclear fission along with <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a>, may have been denied a share of Hahn's 1944 Nobel Chemistry Award due to having fled Germany when the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazis</a> came to power.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The Meitner and Strassmann roles in the research was not fully recognised until years later, when they joined Hahn in receiving the 1966 <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi_Award" title="Enrico Fermi Award">Enrico Fermi Award</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emphasis_on_discoveries_over_inventions">Emphasis on discoveries over inventions</span></h3>
<p>Alfred Nobel left his fortune to finance annual prizes to be awarded "to those who, during the preceding year, shall have conferred the greatest benefit on mankind".<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> He stated that the Nobel Prizes in Physics should be given "to the person who shall have made the most important 'discovery' or 'invention' within the field of physics". Nobel did not emphasise discoveries, but they have historically been held in higher respect by the Nobel Prize Committee than inventions: 77% of the Physics Prizes have been given to discoveries, compared with only 23% to inventions. Christoph Bartneck and Matthias Rauterberg, in papers published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> and <i>Technoetic Arts</i>, have argued this emphasis on discoveries has moved the Nobel Prize away from its original intention of rewarding the greatest contribution to society.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_disparity">Gender disparity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg/220px-The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg/330px-The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg/440px-The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Nobel_Prize_Gender_Gap.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>There have been a total of 57 women Nobel laureates compared to 873 male laureates. Most female laureates received them in the peace and literature categories. Marie Curie was the first female to receive the Nobel Prize in 1903 and the only woman to receive it twice.</div></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_female_Nobel_laureates" title="List of female Nobel laureates">List of female Nobel laureates</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_female_nominees_for_the_Nobel_Prize" title="List of female nominees for the Nobel Prize">List of female nominees for the Nobel Prize</a></div>
<p>In terms of the most prestigious awards in <a href="/wiki/Science,_technology,_engineering,_and_mathematics" title="Science, technology, engineering, and mathematics">STEM</a> fields, only a small proportion have been awarded to women. Out of 210 laureates in Physics, 181 in Chemistry and 216 in Medicine between 1901 and 2018, there were only three female laureates in physics, five in chemistry and 12 in medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Factors proposed to contribute to the discrepancy between this and the roughly equal <a href="/wiki/Human_sex_ratio" title="Human sex ratio">human sex ratio</a> include biased nominations, fewer women than men being active in the relevant fields, Nobel Prizes typically being awarded decades after the research was done (reflecting a time when <a href="/wiki/Gender_bias" class="mw-redirect" title="Gender bias">gender bias</a> in the relevant fields was greater), a greater delay in awarding Nobel Prizes for women's achievements making longevity a more important factor for women (one cannot be nominated to the Nobel Prize posthumously), and a tendency to omit women from jointly awarded Nobel Prizes.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Despite these factors, Marie Curie is to date the only person awarded Nobel Prizes in two different sciences (Physics in 1903, Chemistry in 1911); she is one of only three people who have received two Nobel Prizes in sciences (see Multiple laureates below). <a href="/wiki/Malala_Yousafzai" title="Malala Yousafzai">Malala Yousafzai</a> is the youngest person ever to be awarded the Nobel Peace Prize. When she received it in 2014, she was only 17 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_the_Economic_Sciences_Prize">Status of the Economic Sciences Prize</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Nobel" title="Peter Nobel">Peter Nobel</a> describes the Bank of Sweden Prize in Economic Sciences in Memory of Alfred Nobel as a "false Nobel prize" that dishonours his relative Alfred Nobel, after whom the prize is named, and considers economics to be a pseudoscience.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span></h2>
<ul><li><b>Youngest person to receive a Nobel Prize:</b>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Malala_Yousafzai" title="Malala Yousafzai">Malala Yousafzai</a>; at the age of 17, received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> (2014).</dd></dl></li>
<li><b>Oldest person to receive a Nobel Prize:</b>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/John_B._Goodenough" title="John B. Goodenough">John B. Goodenough</a>; at the age of 97, received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (2019).</dd></dl></li>
<li><b>Only person to receive more than one unshared Nobel Prize:</b>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a>; received the prize twice. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (1954) and <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> (1962).</dd></dl></li>
<li><b>Laureates who have received Multiple Nobel Prizes:</b> (by date of second Prize)
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a>; received the prize twice. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> (1903) and <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (1911).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a>; received the prize three times. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> (1917, 1944, 1963).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a>; received the prize twice. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (1954) and <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> (1962).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">John Bardeen</a>; received the prize twice. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> (1956, 1972).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frederick_Sanger" title="Frederick Sanger">Frederick Sanger</a>; received the prize twice. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (1958, 1980).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees</a>; received the prize twice. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> (1954, 1981).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Karl_Barry_Sharpless" title="Karl Barry Sharpless">Karl Barry Sharpless</a>; received the prize twice. <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (2001, 2022).</li></ol></li>
<li><b>Posthumous Nobel Prizes laureates:</b>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Erik_Axel_Karlfeldt" title="Erik Axel Karlfeldt">Erik Axel Karlfeldt</a>; received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> (1931).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dag_Hammarskj%C3%B6ld" title="Dag Hammarskjöld">Dag Hammarskjöld</a>; received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> (1961).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ralph_M._Steinman" title="Ralph M. Steinman">Ralph M. Steinman</a>; received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> (2011).</li></ol></li>
<li><b>Married couples to receive Nobel Prizes:</b><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><div class="paragraphbreak" style="margin-top:0.5em"></div></li></ul>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_couples_awarded_the_Nobel_Prize" title="List of couples awarded the Nobel Prize">List of couples awarded the Nobel Prize</a></div>
<dl><dd><ol><li><a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a> (along with <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Henri Becquerel</a>). Received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> (1903).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène Joliot-Curie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric Joliot</a>. Received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (1935).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gerty_Cori" title="Gerty Cori">Gerty Cori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Ferdinand_Cori" title="Carl Ferdinand Cori">Carl Cori</a>. Received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> (1947).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Myrdal" title="Gunnar Myrdal">Gunnar Myrdal</a> received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Memorial_Prize_in_Economic_Sciences" title="Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences">Nobel Prize in Economics Sciences</a> (1974), <a href="/wiki/Alva_Myrdal" title="Alva Myrdal">Alva Myrdal</a> received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> (1982).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/May-Britt_Moser" title="May-Britt Moser">May-Britt Moser</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Moser" title="Edvard Moser">Edvard I. Moser</a>. Received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> (2014)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Esther_Duflo" title="Esther Duflo">Esther Duflo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abhijit_Banerjee" title="Abhijit Banerjee">Abhijit Banerjee</a> (along with <a href="/wiki/Michael_Kremer" title="Michael Kremer">Michael Kremer</a>). Received <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Memorial_Prize_in_Economic_Sciences" title="Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences">Nobel Prize in Economics Sciences</a> (2019).<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup></li></ol></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specially_distinguished_laureates">Specially distinguished laureates</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_laureates">Multiple laureates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A black and white portrait of a woman in profile." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg/170px-Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg/255px-Marie_Curie_1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg 2x" data-file-width="280" data-file-height="396" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a>, one of five people who have received the Nobel Prize twice (Physics and Chemistry)</div></div></div>
<p>Five people have received two Nobel Prizes. <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a> received the Physics Prize in 1903 for her work on <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactivity</a> and the Chemistry Prize in 1911 for the isolation of pure <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> making her the only person to be awarded a Nobel Prize in two different sciences. <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a> was awarded the 1954 Chemistry Prize for his research into the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bond</a> and its application to the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_structure" title="Chemical structure">structure</a> of complex substances. Pauling was also awarded the Peace Prize in 1962 for his activism against nuclear weapons, making him the only laureate of two unshared prizes. <a href="/wiki/John_Bardeen" title="John Bardeen">John Bardeen</a> received the Physics Prize twice: in 1956 for the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> and in 1972 for the theory of <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman180_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman180-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Sanger" title="Frederick Sanger">Frederick Sanger</a> received the prize twice in Chemistry: in 1958 for determining the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> molecule and in 1980 for inventing a method of determining base sequences in DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-Shalev78_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shalev78-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feldman222_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman222-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Barry_Sharpless" title="Karl Barry Sharpless">Karl Barry Sharpless</a> was awarded the 2001 Chemistry Prize for his research into chirally catalysed oxidation reactions, and the 2022 Chemistry Prize for <a href="/wiki/Click_chemistry" title="Click chemistry">click chemistry</a>.
</p><p>Two organizations have received the Peace Prize multiple times. The <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> received it three times: in 1917 and 1944 for its work during the world wars; and in 1963 during the year of its centenary.<sup id="cite_ref-Abrams84_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams84-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abrams149_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams149-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abrams199-200_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abrams199-200-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees</a> has been awarded the Peace Prize twice for assisting refugees: in 1954 and 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman313_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman313-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_laureates">Family laureates</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Curie_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="Curie Family">Curie family</a> has received the most prizes, with four prizes awarded to five individual laureates. <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a> received the prizes in Physics (in 1903) and Chemistry (in 1911). Her husband, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a>, shared the 1903 Physics prize with her.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Their daughter, <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène Joliot-Curie</a>, received the Chemistry Prize in 1935 together with her husband <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric Joliot-Curie</a>. In addition, the husband of Marie Curie's second daughter, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Richardson_Labouisse,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Richardson Labouisse, Jr.">Henry Labouisse</a>, was the director of <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> when he accepted the Nobel Peace Prize in 1965 on that organisation's behalf.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although no family matches the Curie family's record, there have been several with two laureates. The Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine was awarded to the husband-and-wife team of <a href="/wiki/Gerty_Cori" title="Gerty Cori">Gerty Cori</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Ferdinand_Cori" title="Carl Ferdinand Cori">Carl Ferdinand Cori</a> in 1947 Prize,<sup id="cite_ref-GrinsteinBiermann1997_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GrinsteinBiermann1997-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> and by the husband-and-wife team of <a href="/wiki/May-Britt_Moser" title="May-Britt Moser">May-Britt Moser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Moser" title="Edvard Moser">Edvard Moser</a> in 2014 (along with <a href="/wiki/John_O%27Keefe_(neuroscientist)" title="John O&#39;Keefe (neuroscientist)">John O'Keefe</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The Physics Prize in 1906 was won by <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> for showing that <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> are particles, and in 1937 by his son, <a href="/wiki/George_Paget_Thomson" title="George Paget Thomson">George Paget Thomson</a>, for showing that they <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave–particle duality">also have the properties of waves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gribbin91_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gribbin91-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Bragg" title="William Henry Bragg">William Henry Bragg</a> and his son, <a href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_Bragg" class="mw-redirect" title="William Lawrence Bragg">William Lawrence Bragg</a>, shared the Physics Prize in 1915 for inventing <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hargittai2003_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hargittai2003-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> was awarded the Physics Prize in 1922, as was his son, <a href="/wiki/Aage_Bohr" title="Aage Bohr">Aage Bohr</a>, in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The Physics Prize was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Manne_Siegbahn" title="Manne Siegbahn">Manne Siegbahn</a> in 1924, followed by his son, <a href="/wiki/Kai_Siegbahn" title="Kai Siegbahn">Kai Siegbahn</a>, in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Euler-Chelpin" title="Hans von Euler-Chelpin">Hans von Euler-Chelpin</a>, who received the Chemistry Prize in 1929, was the father of <a href="/wiki/Ulf_von_Euler" title="Ulf von Euler">Ulf von Euler</a>, who was awarded the Physiology or Medicine Prize in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_179-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C._V._Raman" title="C. V. Raman">C. V. Raman</a> was awarded the Physics Prize in 1930 and was the uncle of <a href="/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar" title="Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar">Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar</a>, who was awarded the same prize in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feldman406_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman406-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Kornberg" title="Arthur Kornberg">Arthur Kornberg</a> received the Physiology or Medicine Prize in 1959; Kornberg's son <a href="/wiki/Roger_D._Kornberg" title="Roger D. Kornberg">Roger</a> later received the Chemistry Prize in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schawlow" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Schawlow">Arthur Schawlow</a> received the 1981 Physics prize, and was married to the sister of 1964 Physics laureate <a href="/wiki/Charles_Townes" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Townes">Charles Townes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Two members of the <a href="/wiki/Hodgkin_family" title="Hodgkin family">Hodgkin family</a> received Nobels in consecutive years: <a href="/wiki/Alan_Hodgkin" title="Alan Hodgkin">Sir Alan Lloyd Hodgkin</a> shared in the Nobel for Physiology or Medicine in 1963, followed by <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Crowfoot_Hodgkin" class="mw-redirect" title="Dorothy Crowfoot Hodgkin">Dorothy Crowfoot Hodgkin</a>, the wife of his first cousin, who won solo for Chemistry in 1964. <a href="/wiki/Jan_Tinbergen" title="Jan Tinbergen">Jan Tinbergen</a>, who was awarded the first Economics Prize in 1969, was the brother of <a href="/wiki/Nikolaas_Tinbergen" title="Nikolaas Tinbergen">Nikolaas Tinbergen</a>, who received the 1973 Physiology or Medicine Prize.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_179-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Myrdal" title="Gunnar Myrdal">Gunnar Myrdal</a> who was awarded the Economics Prize in 1974, was the husband of <a href="/wiki/Alva_Myrdal" title="Alva Myrdal">Alva Myrdal</a>, Peace Prize laureate in 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman405_179-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman405-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Economics laureates <a href="/wiki/Paul_Samuelson" title="Paul Samuelson">Paul Samuelson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Arrow" title="Kenneth Arrow">Kenneth Arrow</a> were brothers-in-law. <a href="/wiki/Frits_Zernike" title="Frits Zernike">Frits Zernike</a>, who was awarded the 1953 Physics Prize, is the great-uncle of 1999 Physics laureate <a href="/wiki/Gerard_%27t_Hooft" title="Gerard &#39;t Hooft">Gerard 't Hooft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, married couple <a href="/wiki/Abhijit_Banerjee" title="Abhijit Banerjee">Abhijit Banerjee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Esther_Duflo" title="Esther Duflo">Esther Duflo</a> were awarded the Economics Prize.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christiane_N%C3%BCsslein-Volhard" title="Christiane Nüsslein-Volhard">Christiane Nüsslein-Volhard</a> was awarded the Prize in Physiology or Medicine in 1995, and her nephew <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_List" title="Benjamin List">Benjamin List</a> received the Chemistry Prize in 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sune_Bergstr%C3%B6m" title="Sune Bergström">Sune Bergström</a> was awarded the Prize in Physiology or Medicine in 1982 and his son <a href="/wiki/Svante_P%C3%A4%C3%A4bo" title="Svante Pääbo">Svante Pääbo</a> in 2022.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Refusals_and_constraints">Refusals and constraints</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A black and white portrait of a man in a suit and tie. Half of his face is in a shadow." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg/170px-Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg/255px-Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg/340px-Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg 2x" data-file-width="401" data-file-height="594" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Kuhn_ETH-Bib_Dia_248-065.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_Kuhn" title="Richard Kuhn">Richard Kuhn</a>, who was forced to decline his Nobel Prize in Chemistry</div></div></div>
<p>Two laureates have voluntarily declined the Nobel Prize. In 1964, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a> was awarded the Literature Prize but refused, stating, "A writer must refuse to allow himself to be transformed into an institution, even if it takes place in the most honourable form."<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/L%C3%AA_%C4%90%E1%BB%A9c_Th%E1%BB%8D" title="Lê Đức Thọ">Lê Đức Thọ</a>, chosen for the 1973 Peace Prize for his role in the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Accords" title="Paris Peace Accords">Paris Peace Accords</a>, declined, stating that there was no actual peace in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> attempted to decline the prize money while accepting the 1925 Literature Prize; eventually it was agreed to use it to found the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Swedish_Literary_Foundation" title="Anglo-Swedish Literary Foundation">Anglo-Swedish Literary Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Third Reich, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> hindered <a href="/wiki/Richard_Kuhn" title="Richard Kuhn">Richard Kuhn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Butenandt" title="Adolf Butenandt">Adolf Butenandt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Domagk" title="Gerhard Domagk">Gerhard Domagk</a> from accepting their prizes. All of them were awarded their diplomas and gold medals after World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1958, <a href="/wiki/Boris_Pasternak" title="Boris Pasternak">Boris Pasternak</a> declined his prize for literature due to fear of what the Soviet Union government might do if he travelled to Stockholm to accept his prize. In return, the Swedish Academy refused his refusal, saying "this refusal, of course, in no way alters the validity of the award."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The academy announced with regret that the presentation of the Literature Prize could not take place that year, holding it back until 1989 when Pasternak's son accepted the prize on his behalf.<sup id="cite_ref-timesonline-292690_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline-292690-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-washingtonpost_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aung_San_Suu_Kyi" title="Aung San Suu Kyi">Aung San Suu Kyi</a> was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in 1991, but her children accepted the prize because she had been placed under house arrest in <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Burma</a>; Suu Kyi delivered her speech two decades later, in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liu_Xiaobo" title="Liu Xiaobo">Liu Xiaobo</a> was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in 2010 while he and his wife were under house arrest in China as political prisoners, and he was unable to accept the prize in his lifetime.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span></h2>
<p>Being a symbol of scientific or literary achievement that is recognisable worldwide, the Nobel Prize is often depicted in fiction. This includes films like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prize_(1963_film)" title="The Prize (1963 film)">The Prize</a></i> (1963), <i><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Son" title="Nobel Son">Nobel Son</a></i> (2007), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wife_(2017_film)" title="The Wife (2017 film)">The Wife</a></i> (2017) about fictional Nobel laureates, as well as fictionalised accounts of stories surrounding real prizes such as <i><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Chor" title="Nobel Chor">Nobel Chor</a></i>, a 2012 film based on the <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore#Theft_of_Nobel_Prize" title="Rabindranath Tagore">theft of Rabindranath Tagore's prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nobel_film_screen_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_film_screen-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The statue and memorial symbol <i>Planet of Alfred Nobel</i> was opened in <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Nobel_University" title="Alfred Nobel University">Alfred Nobel University</a> of Economics and Law in <a href="/wiki/Dnipro" title="Dnipro">Dnipro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> in 2008. On the globe, there are 802 Nobel laureates' reliefs made of a composite alloy obtained when disposing of military strategic missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the symbolism of intellectual achievement, some recipients have embraced unsupported and <a href="/wiki/Pseudoscience" title="Pseudoscience">pseudoscientific</a> concepts, including various <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C_and_the_Common_Cold_(book)#Research,_writing_and_revisions" title="Vitamin C and the Common Cold (book)">health benefits of vitamin C</a> and other dietary supplements, <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_denialism" title="HIV/AIDS denialism">HIV/AIDS denialism</a>, and various claims about <a href="/wiki/Race_and_intelligence" title="Race and intelligence">race and intelligence</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> This is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Nobel_disease" title="Nobel disease">Nobel disease</a>.
</p>
